T 1709838147 18<20leftyfb18>	keyboard shortcuts are a 2 part process
T 1709838202 18<20leftyfb18>	gsettings set org.gnome.settings-daemon.plugins.media-keys custom-keybindings "['/org/gnome/settings-daemon/plugins/media-keys/custom-keybindings/custom0/']"
T 1709838233 18<20leftyfb18>	that's part 1
T 1709838244 18<20leftyfb18>	then defining the details of the custom sortuct
T 1709838246 18<20leftyfb18>	shortcut*
T 1709838253 18<20leftyfb18>	    - gsettings set org.gnome.settings-daemon.plugins.media-keys.custom-keybinding:/org/gnome/settings-daemon/plugins/media-keys/custom-keybindings/custom0/ name "nautilus"
T 1709838254 18<20leftyfb18>	    - gsettings set org.gnome.settings-daemon.plugins.media-keys.custom-keybinding:/org/gnome/settings-daemon/plugins/media-keys/custom-keybindings/custom0/ command "nautilus"
T 1709838254 18<20leftyfb18>	    - gsettings set org.gnome.settings-daemon.plugins.media-keys.custom-keybinding:/org/gnome/settings-daemon/plugins/media-keys/custom-keybindings/custom0/ binding "<Super>e"
T 1709838273 18<29atreo18>	leftyfb: ohhh   ok i think i see how this works
T 1709838288 18<29atreo18>	ok let me try to understand what you just wrote     give me 5 minutes :-)
T 1709838318 18<20leftyfb18>	atreo: if you add another shortcut, you need to run the 1st command again but append the 2nd custom shortcut (custom1)

T 1710097842 18<26ic5618>	bartosz: the traditional unix tools for such tasks are find(1), ls(1), and file(1).  The choice depends on your preferred output format.  For example, supposing the "disk" in question is mounted at /media/usb0 , you might try: cd /media/usb0 && find . |sed 's|./||' |sort  The sort(1) command is necessary because find(1), unlike ls(1), traverses directories as written to disk, without sorting.  The sed(1)
T 1710097848 18<26ic5618>	command reduces the output.  There are faster ways to accomplish that; this is more legible for a novice.  The other option, "ls -lR", produces pretty output but not helpful for searches.  file(1) can be used in a loop to produce information about each file.  Eg save the sorted find(1) output to a file, then do something like this: (while read j; do printf '%s\t' $j; file $j; done) <f1 >f2
